ON   SOME  PASSAGES  IN  GOETHE'S  "FAUST"  AND 

THEIR  INTERPRETATION  BY  PROFESSOR 

CALVIN  THOMAS. 


The  revised  edition  of  Goethe's  Faitst^  by  Professor  Thomas, 
is  an  improvement,  bnt  still  contains  a  few  misinterpretations 
which  cannot  be  disposed  of  by  the  editor's  general  remark  in 
the  preface  (p.  v.)  that  "  Faust  teems  with  words  and  phrases  the 
meaning  of  which  is  uncertain  and  which  are  differently  under- 
stood by  the  most  competent  experts." 

"  Teems  "  is  a  strong  word  and  scarcely  fits  the  occasion.  The 
first  part  of  Faust,  in  particular,  is  not  difficult  reading,  in  so 
far  as  the  meaning  of  the  luords  is  concerned.  The  exact  inter- 
pretation of  the  meaning  of  certain  passages  is  another  matter, 
but  even  these  are  not  so  very  numerous  in  the  first  part  as  to 
constitute  a  necessity  for  extensive  comment.  But  the  work  is 
intensely  idiomatic,  and  if  one  forgets  this  fact,  one  may  find 
difficulties  where  there  are  none. 

Where  uncertainty  really  exists  the  editor  has,  as  a  rule,  done 
very  well,  but  there  are  a  few  cases,  and  they  are  of  some  impor- 
tance, where  it  would  be  wrong,  in  my  opinion,  to  speak  of 
uncertainty. 

I  agree  fully  with  Professor  Thomas  as  to  what  should  be  the 
one  great  purpose  of  the  editor's  notes  on  a  classic,  "  that  it 
should  be  to  help  the  reader  enter  more  perfectly  than  he  other- 
wise might  into  the  thought  and  feeling  of  the  author,"  and  I 
sincerely  compliment  the  editor  of  Fansi  on  his  success.  But  I 
demur  strongly,  for  instance,  to  his  spoiling,  for  the  student,  the 
striking  force  and  beauty  of  the  metaphor  in  line  141  (Part  I). 

To  make  my  meaning  clear  I  will  quote  the  entire  passage ; 

(62) 


Charles  A.  Eggert.  63 

"  Der  Dichter  soUte  wol  das  hochste  Recht, 
Das  Menschenrecht,  das  ihm  Natur  vergonnt, 
Um  deinetwillen  freventlich  verscherzen  ! 
Wodurch  bewegt  er  alle  Herzen  ? 
1st  es  der  Einklang  nicht,  der  aus  dem  Busen  dringt, 
Und  in  sein  Herz  die  Welt  zuriicke  sclilingt  ? ' ' 

The  editor  explains  :  zuriicke  schlingt  :=  devour.  He  quotes 
from  Goethe's  Tasso  to  support  his  view  . 

"  Sein  Ohr  vernimmt  den  Einklang  der  Natur  ; 
Was  die  Geschichte  reicht,  das  Eeben  giebt, 
Sein  Busen  nimmt  es  gleich  und  willig  auf : 
Das  weit  Zerstreute  sammelt  sein  Gemiit 
Und  sein  Gefiihl  belebt  das  Unbelebte. ' ' 

Here  the  "Einklang"  is  in  nature.  It  enters  the  poet's 
heart  together  with  the  lessons  of  history  and  life.  His  mind 
"  collects  "  what  is  scattered,  and  his  feeling  gives  life  to  the 
lifeless.  This  simile  is  quite  different  from  the  one  quoted 
before,  less  striking,  though  just  as  apt,  and  without  the  remotest 
idea  of  such  a  process  of  vulgar  "  gulping  down  "  as  the  word 
"  schlingen  "  implies  when  it  is  translated  by  "  devour." 

The  poet  was  very  far  from  using  it  in  this  vulgar  sense.  He 
personifies  "Einklang"  as  something  that  exists  in  his  bosom, 
forces  its  way  out  into  the  world  and  returns  to  whence  it  came 
with  what  it  found  in  the  world.  We  are  reminded  of  a  lasso 
that  is  hurled  forth  and  then  drawn  back  \A\h  the  captured 
victim. 

Kluge  defines  schlingen  (M.  H.  G.  slingen  ;  O.  H.  G.  slingan) 
as  equivalent  to  "  winden,  flechten,  hin  und  herziehend  schwin- 
gen^  It  is  quite  the  same  in  derivation,  and  closely  allied  with 
it  in  meaning,  as  A.  S.  slmgan^  Engl.  sHng^  O.  N.  slyngva,  and 
corresponds  to  a  Gothic  *slingwan  (*sleihwan).  Even  its  root 
slingw  (cp.  schlenkern,  Schlange)  or  "^slinhw^  from  pre-Germanic 
slenk^  contains  the  idea  of  a  turning,  twisting  or  swinging  move- 
ment. So  we  have  in  M.  H.  G.  slenge^  sleiiger^  sleuker  =  N.  H.  G. 
Sckleuder,  from  the  root  sling,  whence  O.  H.  G.  slinga,  M.  H.  G. 


64  On  Some  Passages  in  Goethe's  ^' Faust.'" 

slinge,  English  sling.     (Cp.  also  French  elinque  from  the  same 
root.) 

Hence  in  all  its  significations,  as  far  as  we  can  trace  the  word, 
it  conveys  the  idea  of  a  motion,  a  twisting  or  weaving,  and 
closely  connected  with  it,  the  idea  of  throwing  or  hurling  a  rope 
for  the  purpose  of  catching  and  fastening.  The  modern  Ger- 
man Schlifige.,  a  snare  to  entrap  birds,  etc.,  and  the  English 
sling  (German  Schleiider)  express  the  noun  which  forms  the 
stem  of  the  verb  used  by  Goethe  in  the  passage  quoted.* 

We  may  further  illustrate  the  force  of  the  meaning  of  the 
word  by  the  various  uses  to  which  it  has  been  put  by  the  poets. 
We  find  "  ein  Band  der  Freundschaft  schlingen  ; "  "von  Liebe 
und  Freundschaft  umschlungen  sein  ;  "  "  umschlingen  "  is  "  to 
clasp  "  or  "  embrace  "  in  Schiller's  "  Seid  umschlungen  Milli- 
onen."  These  expressions,  and  Goethe's  "  Dass  die  Freude  :  .  . 
den  schonsten  Kranz  von  Saul  an  Saulen  schlinge,"  all  readily 
suggest  the  true  meaning  of  "  schlingen "  in  this  passage. 
Schlingen  has  the  extremely  vulgar  meaning  of  "  devour  "  only 
in  low  and  familiar  language.  But  in  this  passage  the  poet 
speaks  with  the  authority  of  the  favorite  of  Apollo,  as  a  seer 
and  prophet.  The  whole  passage,  with  its  sequel,  is  an  antithesis 
to  all  that  is  vulgar.  It  is  in  fact,  perhaps,  the  noblest  vindica- 
tion of  the  poet's  mission,  and  poetic  to  the  very  core. 

The  passage  illustrates  the  difficulty  of  correctly  translating 
the  subtle  conception  of  a  poet.  In  the  German  "  der  Einklang," 
by  virtue  of  the  definite  article,  stands  forth  clearly  and  distinctly 
as  a  personification.  As  such  it  is  presented  as  "  issuing  "  forth 
and,  by  an  encircling  motion,  bringing  back  what  it  caught.  In 
English,  when  we  translate  "der  Einklang"  by  "  harmonious 
accent "  we  cannot  get  rid  of  the  exact  meaning  of  the  word, 
hence  we  fail  to  reproduce  the  original  picture.  An  "accent" 
or  "sound,"  or  even  the  more  abstract  term  "harmony,"  does 
not  convey  the  idea  of  a  personality  or  of  an  instrument  that 
can  serve   to   encircle    or  envelop.     And  yet,  English   poetr}- 

*  The  vulgar  meaning  of  schlingen  has  a  dififerent  derivation,  from  M.  H.  C. 
%linden,  O.  H.  G.  slintan,  "  swallow  up. " 


Charles  A.  Eggert.  65 

takes  the  same  liberties,  and  parallel  cases  might  be  cited 
from  Shakespeare,  Byron  and  Tennyson. 

The  "  Da "  in  line  268  the  editor  interprets  as  meaning 
"since."  He  explains:  '''•since  the  Lord's  nature  is  unfathom- 
able, the  contemplation  of  his  works  affords  the  angels  an  ever 
fresh  source  of  strength.  What  one  understands  perfectly  becomes 
in  time  an  old  story^  The  words  which  I  have  put  in  italics 
express,  I  believe,  such  a  misconception  of  the  passage  as 
amounts  to  a  failure  to  appreciate  its  poetry.  I  am  inclined 
to  think  that  no  poet  is  so  free  from  commonplaces  as  Goethe. 
It  would  sound  very  much  like  a  commonplace  for  an  angel  to 
say  what  is  implied  in  Professor  Thomas'  interpretation  :  "  If 
the  Lord  were  not  so  hard  to  understand,  I  should  long  since 
have  become  tired  of  Him."  The  angel  lays  the  stress  on  the 
never  ceasing  glory  of  the  works  of  God.  He  says  :  "  While 
none  can  fathom  Thee,  yet  Thy  sight  gives  strength  to  the 
angels,  and  Thy  inconceivably  lofty  works  are  as  glorious  now 
as  they  were  when  first  created."  We  might  amplify  the 
meaning  by  interpreting :  "  If  we  could  fathom  the  deity  we 
should  be  incredibly  blessed,  but  even  without  this  capacity  the 
mere  sight  of  Him  gives  strength  to  us."  And  further: 
"Though  Thy  lofty  works  have  stood  for  ages,  yet  they  are 
ever  fresh  and  inspiring  as  on  the  day  when  Thy  voice  called 
them  into  existence." 

"Da"  may,  of  course,  mean  "since,"  and  this  is  its  obvious 
meaning  in  sentences  of  inference.  But  Goethe's  use  of  "da" 
is  frequently  that  of  the  more  modern  "als"  =  when^  or  even 
while.     So  in  "  Iphigenie  auf  Tauris : " 

' '  Da  alle  Rettung  auf  der  weiten  Erde 
Verloren  schien,  giebst  du  uns  alias  wieder." 

(Compare  also,  1.  c,  11.  636,  645,  2132.) 

Schroer  is  therefore  justified  in  considering  the  passage  as 
concessive  and  in  quoting  line  258,  "  Wenn  Keiner  dich  er- 
griinden  mag  "  as  parallel.  It  is  true,  however,  that  "  da  "  in 
the  concessive  sense,  and  as  equivalent  to  "als,"  is  pretty  regu- 

6 


66  071  Some  Passages  in  Goethe's  '^ Faust." 

larly  used  by  Goethe  in  the  past  tense,  wenn  in  the  present  and 
future.  We  should  therefore  expect  "  wenn  "  in  line  268,  and 
might  claim,  with  the  editor,  that  the  meaning  of  "da"  must  be 
"  since,"  though  not  for  the  reason  he  gives,  if  such  a  translation 
did  not  detract  both  from  the  poetry  and  the  significance  of  the 
passage.  Why  should  the  imfathomableness  of  the  deity  be  a 
source  of  stj'ength  to  the  beholder?  Strehlke's  explanation 
that  "  da  "  is  here  equal  to  "  da  wo  "  shows  him  to  discounte- 
nance the  idea  that  it  stands  for  "since."  It  seems  that  Pro- 
fessor Thomas  stands  alone  in  giving  it  this  meaning. 

I  cannot  quite  agree  with  Professor  Thomas  in  what  he  says 
about  the  difficulty  of  II.  345-49.  I  think  his  own  explanation, 
except  in  one  respect,  suffices  to  show  that  the  thought  expressed 
is  both  capable  of  being  translated  and  of  being  logically 
analyzed.  The  Lord  addresses  the  angels  and  says  in  conclusion: 

' '  Und  was  in  schwankender  Erscheinung  schwebt 
Befestiget  mit  dauernden  Gedanken." 

Goethe  seems  to  identify  here  the  angels,  as  the  sons  of  God, 
with  beings  who  are  akin  to  the  human  poet.  His  splendid 
eulogy  of  the  poet  and  his  mission  in  the  first  part  of  Wilhelm 
Meister  (p.  71  of  H.  Diintzer's  edition,  in  the  Kiirschner-Cotta 
series,  vol.  xv)  contains  expressions  which  point  in  the  same 
direction.  He  compares  the  poet  to  a  "god,"  to  a  "wandelnde 
Sonne,"  and  calls  him  "a  friend  of  the  gods,"  We  must  also 
bear  in  mind  that  the  word  "poet"  suggests  production;  but 
production  in  the  modern  sense  is  only  change,  a  rearranging  of 
the  elements  which  have  always  existed.  Now,  while  all  that 
is  produced  will  finally  change  again,  hence  die,  it  is  not  so  with 
the  production  of  the  poet.  He  "  fixes  "  the  fleeting  atoms  into 
a  permanent  form.  In  this  sense  the  only  permanent  product  is 
the  work  of  the  poet,  who  gives  a  form  to  that  "  which  hovers 
in  the'undulating  world  of  phenomena  "  ("  was  in  schwankender 
Erscheinung  schwebt  ").  The  poet  does  in  this  world  what  the 
angels  are  doing  in  the  world  above  in  obedience  to  the  divine 
command. 


Charles  A.  Eggert.  67 

This  special  office  of  the  poet  was  similarly  conceived  of  by 
Shakespeare  when  he  says  of  him  that  "he  gives  to  airy  noth- 
ings a  local  habitation  and  a  name."  Tlie  thought  also  under- 
lies Schiller's  lines  : 

"  Nur  was  nie  und  nirgend  sich  begeben, 
Das  allein  veraltet  nie. ' ' 

L.  651  deserves  a  note.  What  does  the  poet  mean  ?  "  You 
must  forever  bewail  what  you  never  lose?"  is  certainly  not  a 
self-evident  proposition. 

Iv.  1237  contains  one  of  the  profoundest  of  Goethe's  sayings. 
"  Im  Anfang  war  die  That."  Professor  Thomas  says :  "  Strictly 
he  (?'.  <?.,  Faust)  should  regret  this  also,  for  a  '  deed '  implies  a 
doer."  Humanly  speaking,  it  does;  but  the  application  is  here 
to  the  divine  mind.  God  has  no  need  to  conceive  and  formu- 
late ;  with  Him  thought  and  action  are  identical.  He  thinks 
in  trees  and  flowers,  in  suns  and  solar  systems.  This  elimina- 
tion of  the  human  apparatus  for  the  purpose  of  divine  action  is 
admirable.  It  is  the  nearest  approach  to  the  conception  of  the 
nature  of  Deity,  and  worth  whole  libraries  of  theological  elucu- 
brations. 

But  is  not  there  something  more  in  this  scene  than  the  notes 
allow  us  to  surmise  ?  I  believe  there  is  here  a  humorous  trait 
that  escapes  most  readers,  and  something  that  is  more  than  mere 
humor. 

Into  the  hallowed  stillness  of  his  study-room  Faust  has 
returned  with  feelings  of  noble  aspiration.  In  such  a  mood  he 
must  be  very  disagreeable  to  the  devil  whose  special  object  is 
"to  draw  him  away  from  his  Urquell "  (I.  324).  Whenever 
Faust  draws  a  step  nearer  to  the  fountain  of  true  happiness, 
when  his  mind  becomes  pure  and  clear,  when  his  passions  rest 
and  his  faith  revives,  his  companion  endures  acute  pain  (II.  11 86, 
1201,  1238-40).  The  humorous  part,  unless  I  am  greatly  mis- 
taken, is  in  the  extreme  anguish  of  the  poodle  when  Faust 
translates  lbyo(^  by  deed^  thus  cutting  loose  from  a  servile  word- 
translation  of  the  sacred  passage,  and  resorting  to  a  free  and 
spiritual  mode  of  interpretation.     This  the  devil  cannot  possibly 


68  Oil  Some  Passages  in  Goethe's  ^'  Faust.'" 

endure.     His  hope  of  success   is  expressed   further  on  in   the 
significant  lines  (1851-55) : 

"  Verachte  nur  Vernunft  und  Wissenschaft, 
Des  Menschen  allerhochste  Kraft " 

To  make  Faust  doubt  the  sovereignty  of  "  Vernunft  und 
Wissenschaft,"  to  make  him  turn  his  attention  to  charlatanism 
and  low  pleasures  is  the  policy  which  Mephistopheles  tries  to 
pursue.  He  is  momentarily  thwarted  by  the  reassertion  of 
Faust's  better  nature:  hence  his  torment* 

The  editor  does  not  seem  to  have  laid  sufficient  stress  on  the 
possibility  of  a  reasonable  explanation  of  the  difficulties  as  to 
time.  When  Gretchen  plucks  the  Sternblume  we  are  at  the  end 
of  summer,  when  the  asters  appear.  The  Brocken  scene  must 
then  be  put  a  year  after  the  opening  of  the  play,  and  this  would 
reconcile  some  seeming  inconsistencies,  f 

Faust  has  been  led  by  the  devil, — that  is  by  his  sensual  pas- 
sions, to  which  he  gave  way  when  despairing  to  reach  the  high 
goal  of  his  earlier  ambition, — to  commit  a  great  wrong.  In 
spite  of  the  coarse  revelry  of  the  Walpurgisnacht  his  thoughts 
turn  again  to  Gretchen,  whose  fate  has  been  meanwhile  drawing 
toward  its  end.     Keeping  this  in   mind  the  II.  4183-88,  and 

*  Professor  Thomas  calls  these  lines  a  "useless  and  incongruous  soliloquy.'''' 
For  me  they  contain  the  text  for  the  whole  of  Goethe's  Faust,  that  is,  of  the 
Faust  as  the  poet  wrought  the  work  in  the  best  years  of  his  riper  manhood.  It 
is  really  immaterial  what  original  intentions  he  may  have  had.  What  we  are 
interested  in  is  the  poet's  intention  as  actually  revealed  in  the  finished  work. 
All  the  rest  is  either  learned  or  speculative  pastime,  or  useless  guessing. 
Goethe's  intense  interest  in  the  progress  of  scientific  thought  fully  explains  the 
intention  of  the  passage.  Faust  is  finally  saved  only  because,  amid  all  his 
follies  and  mistakes,  he  never  succeeded  in  stifling  the  inner  voice  that  told 
him  of  the  right  ivay.  cf.  1.  328-29. 

t  Gretchen's  seduction  must  be  placed  in  August.  Hence  her  child  is  born 
in  May.  Faust  is  compelled  to  flee  after  the  death  of  Valentine, — this  explains 
the  Walpurgisnachts  scene,  if  the  activity  of  Mephistopheles  be  taken  into  ac- 
count. Did  the  poet  intend  to  represent  Gretchen  as  having  sinned  but  once  ? 
Professor  Thomas  rejects  the  idea,  in  spite  of  the  II.  12066-7  of  the  second  part. 
(Introd.  p.  Ixii).  But  we  may  still  plead  that  the  poet's  evident  intention  must 
be  respected,  even  in  spite  of  some  difficulties  of  complete  explanations.  A 
drama  is  not  a  lawsuit. 


Charles  A.  Eggert.  69 

4195-98,  acquire  a  mournful  pathos  that  prepares  us  for  the 
unequaled  pathetic  scene  in  the  prison. 

Professor  Thomas,  it  might  seem,  sees  less  in  these  scenes 
with  Gretchen  in  the  foreground  than  the  poet  himself  did. 
Nowhere  has  Goethe  worked  with  such  intensity  of  feeling  as 
when  he  composed  these  scenes.  That  is  proven  by  the  irre- 
sistible effect  they  have  on  minds  not  blase  or  depraved. 

But  it  is  not  merely  the  pathos  of  these  final  scenes,  it  is  also, 
and  largely,  the  moral  earnestness  of  the  poet  that  asserts  itself 
here.  No  great  writer  or  poet  was  more  deeply  convinced  that 
there  is  d.  Nemesis  to  punish  crime  ;  that  there  is  no  escape  from 
the  consequences  of  wrongdoing,  be  that  wrongdoing  a  violation 
of  simple  reason  and  logic,  or  of  the  dictates  of  conscience  and 
the  moral  law.  Faust  has  sunk  very  low  ;  he  has  brought  end- 
less misery  on  beings  who,  but  for  him,  would  have  been  content 
and  happy.  In  a  poetically  exaggerated  form,  but  with  an  over- 
powering pathos,  the  poet  has  painted  his  own  wrong  in  the 
desertion  of  poor  Friederike  Brion.  The  world  has  long  since 
acquitted  the  young  student  of  the  severe  charge  he  laid  against 
himself  as  a  poet  and  a  man.  He  was  too  young,  too  dependent 
on  his  father  to  have  married  the  rustic  maiden  of  Sesenheim, 
and  his  subsequent  admiration  of  Fran  von  Stein  would  seem  to 
prove  that  a  union  with  Friederike  might  not  have  been  the  best 
thing  for  both.  But  the  poet  never  forgot  his  own  share  in  the 
sad  affair,  and  into  the  touching  figure  of  Gretchen  he  put  all 
the  loveliness  he  had  found  in  Friederike.  It  seems  to  me, 
therefore,  that  some  comment  on  this,  the  moral  side  of  the 
question,  which  the  final  scenes  of  the  tragedy  raise,  would  not 
have  been  out  of  place.  For  in  spite  of  all  that  may  be  said  in 
favor  of  the  second  part,  the  poetical  and  ethical  value  of  the 
first  part  of  Faust  is  so  great  and  so  genuine  that  the  second 
part  pales,  in  comparison,  into  the  sober  splendor  of  a  picturesque 
ice  mountain.* 

*  The  punishment  Faust  suflFersin  his  conscience;  there  could  have  been  none 
greater.  It  would  demonstrate  an  utter  failure  of  appreciating  this  higher 
form  of  condign  retribution  to  retort  that  it  is  Gretcheu  alone  who  suffers,  and 
that  Faust  escapes  unhurt  and  unpunished. 


yo  On  Some  Passages  in  Goethe's  ^'  Fatist.'" 

It  is  an  ungrateful  task  to  call  attention  to  a  few  isolated 
points  that  one  cannot  fully  approve,  when  there  is  so  much  in 
the  edition  that  calls  for  high  praise.  I  take  it  for  granted, 
however,  that  allowance  will  be  made  for  my  endeavor  to  point 
out  and  to  explain  some  features  that  are  really  of  very  great  im- 
portance. It  is  not  my  intention  to  find  fault,  but  to  help  in 
clearing  away  some  misunderstandings. 

Professor  Thomas  clings  to  the  old  orthography  in  the  form 
in  which  Professor  Litzmann  has  adopted  it  in  his  edition.  This 
is  a  matter  of  taste,  I  believe,  not  of  scholarship.  A  simplifica- 
tion of  spelling  on  rational  lines  is  the  great  desideratum  in  all 
languages.  The  Prussian  improved  orthography,  though  not 
yet  what  a  perfectly  rational  orthography  ought  to  be,  is  a  great 
improvement  on  the  older  form — why  not  use  it  then  ?  The 
student  is  entitled  to  the  best,  at  least  to  what  is  most  in  use, 
and  there  is  no  question  as  to  the  overwhelming  predominance 
in  German  publications  of  the  new  spelling. 

What  would  we  think  of  an  editor  who  would  force  the 
Shakespeare  of  the  seventeenth  or  eighteenth  century  on  us? 
or  even  of  twenty  years  ago,  if  meanwhile  an  improvement  had 
been  made  in  English  spelling?  There  is  no  wrong  done  to 
Goethe,  any  more  than  to  Shakespeare,  if  we  print  his  works 
in  the  latest  style  of  spelling,  for  we  certainly  do  not  think  of 
printing  them  in  the  style  in  which  these  poets  themselves 
wrote  them.  If  we  change  from  their  spelling  at  all,  why  not 
change  to  the  full  extent  of  the  best  modern  usage  ? 

Charles  A.  Eggert. 

CHICAGO,   SEPTEMBER,    24,    1898. 


THE    GOETHE     INSTITUTIONS    AT    WEIMAR    AND 

THEIR   WORK   FROM    THE   BEGINNING   OF 

1897  TO  THE  MIDDLE  OF  1898. 


The  movement  which  has  resulted  in  the  foundation  of  the 
Goethe  institutions  at  Weimar  was  given  its  impulse  by  the 
descendants  of  the  members  of  the  famous  Weimar  circle  of  the 
last  century.  The  last  grandson  of  Goethe  willed  Goethe's 
estate  to  the  State  and  to  the  wife  of  the  grandson  of  Carl 
August ;  the  grandson  and  great-grandson  of  Schiller  added 
what  they  possessed  of  Schiller's  books  and  manuscripts  ;  their 
example  was  followed  by  a  grandson  of  Herder,  two  great-grand- 
children of  Wieland  and  a  grand-nephew  of  Luise  von  Goech- 
hausen ;  other  gifts  were  made  by  a  great-granddaughter  of  Frau 
von  Stein  and  a  grandson  of  "  Euphrosyne."  Interest  was 
aroused  and  sustained  in  Germany  and  Austria  and  far  beyond 
their  boundaries.  The  Goethe  Society,  under  the  presidency 
of  the  same  man  who  had  presided  over  the  German  National 
Parliament  of  Frankfurt  in  1848,  soon  numbered  over  three 
thousand  members,  among  them  160  from  England  and  eighty 
from  this  country.  The  "  Goethehaus,"  transformed  into  a 
"  Goethe-National-Museum,"  commenced  to  see  visitors  from  all 
lands.  The  "Goethe-  und  Schiller-Archiv  "  began  to  receive  the 
literary  estates  of  post-classical  poets,  such  as  Ludwig  Bechstein, 
Freiligrath,  Hebbel,  Immermann,  Otto  Ludwigand  Fritz  Renter. 
The  monumental  edition  of  Goethes  Werke  "  herausgegebe7i  im 
Auftrage  der  Grossherzogin  Sophie  v.  5*.,"  was  pushed  vigor- 
ously with  a  staff  of  over  sixty  editors.  The  Goethe  Jahrhich 
and  the  Schriften  der  Goethe  Gesellschaft  brought  out  valuable 
unpublished  material  and  discussions.  The  lectures  on  Goethe 
and  on  classical  German  literature  in  the  universities  multiplied 
and  new  efforts  were  made  by  the  theatre,  especially  that  of 
Weimar,  to  revive  classical  plays.      Family  after  family,  indi- 

(71) 


72  The  Goethe  Institidions  at   Wehnar. 

vidiial  after  individual,  continued  to  bequeath,  donate  or  loan 
their  dearest  treasures  to  the  museum  or  the  archives.  Grand 
Duchess  Sophie  and  Grand  Duke  Carl  Alexander,  with  the 
co-operation  of  the  Goethe  Society,  made  many  important  pur- 
chases and  rescued  one  precious  relic  after  another  from  the 
hands  of  speculators.  The  Grand  Duchess  completed  the 
beautiful  new  home  of  the  archives  on  the  right  bank  of  the 
Ilm,  overlooking  town,  castle  and  river.  The  inauguration  was 
made  doubly  memorable  by  the  acquisition  of  the  seven  volumes 
of  Goethe's  letters  to  Frau  von  Stein. 

Thus  the  year  1897  began  with  the  brightest  prospects. 
Unfortunately,  however,  early  in  spring  Grand  Duchess  Sophie, 
the  patroness  of  the  archives,  fell  ill  and  soon  passed  away.  Yet 
she  had  not  died  without  providing  for  her  institution.  This 
she  had  left  to  her  grandson,  the  heir  to  the  throne,  and  her 
determination  was  that  ever  afterward  it  should  be  transmitted 
as  an  inalienable  family  possession  from  one  head  of  the  dynasty 
to  the  next,  and  that  it  be  administrated  for  the  benefit  of  con- 
temporaries and  posterity.  The  new  owner  asked  Grand  Duke 
Carl  August  to  act  as  a  protector  while  he  lived,  a  request  which 
was  gladly  and  faithfully  complied  with.  The  Grand  Duke, 
who  is  one  of  the  few  persons  living  who  have  personal  recollec- 
tions of  Goethe,  visits  the  archives  every  week  and  takes  an 
active  interest  in  all  its  concerns.  The  regular  spring  conven- 
tion of  the  Goethe  Society  was  omitted  out  of  respect  to  the 
memory  of  the  late  Grand  Duchess  Sophie,  and  instead  a  com- 
memorative meeting  was  held  on  the  day  which  would  have  been 
the  fifty-fifth  anniversary  of  her  wedding. 

During  the  first  part  of  1898  occurred  a  memorial  exhibition 
in  the  archives,  the  convention  of  the  Goethe  Society  and  the 
eightieth  birthday  of  Carl  Alexander.  On  the  eve  of  the  con- 
vention a  son  of  Victor  von  Scheffel  deposited  his  father's  lit- 
erary estate  and,  on  the  birthday  of  the  Grand  Duke,  Emperor 
William  promised  to  donate  photographic  reproductions  of  all 
the  Goethe  manuscripts  in  the  Royal  Library  of  Berlin. 

The   Goethe-National-Museum    consists  of    the   Goethehaus, 


Adolf  Gerher.  73 

on  the  Goetheplatz,  and  its  collections.  The  thorough  renova- 
tion of  the  building,  which  was  found  necessary  when  the  State 
took  charge  of  it  and  was  afterward  carried  out  in  a  most  cir- 
cumspect  and  conservative  manner,  has  proved  a  complete  suc- 
cess. We  ascend  first  part  of  the  broad  staircase  in  order  to  see 
Goethe's  study  and  bedroom  in  the  rear  part  of  the  house,  the 
former  with  two  windows  looking  out  into  the  garden  and  the 
latter  with  one  in  the  same  direction.  An  inventory  made 
immediately  after  Goethe's  death  is  proof  that  they  are  practi- 
cally as  they  were  on  the  morning  when  he  breathed  his  last. 
Indeed,  we  feel  as  if  the  spirit  of  the  poet  were  still  moving  in 
the  twilight  of  this  sanctuary  All  luxury,  nay,  even  comfort, 
was  banished  hence  because  here  was  lived  a  life  hostile  to  all 
mere  show  and  idle  ease,  a  life  devoted  to  that  imceasing  activity 
by  which  it  and  all  mankind  were  lifted  to  ever  higher  spheres 
of  thought  and  wisdom.  Here  was  completed  the  second  part 
of  Faiist^  his  bequest  to  posterity,  and  in  the  seat  over  yonder 
the  dying  poet  is  said  to  have  murmured  his  memorable  "  Mehr 
Ivicht !  Mehr  Licht !  "  A  few  more  steps  lead  to  the  long  suite  of 
reception  and  other  rooms  on  the  second  floor  of  the  main  house. 
They  present  an  abundance  of  busts,  pictures  and  objets  (Tart 
collected  by  Goethe  or  referring  to  him  and  his  life.  There  are 
also  a  few  relics,  none  of  which  is  more  touching  than  a  tiny 
bunch  of  dry  flowers  accompanied  by  a  few  lines  recently  writ- 
ten by  the  hand  of  Ulrike  von  Levetzow,  now  a  canoness  in 
Triblic  castle,  Bohemia,  saying  that  they  are  the  last  of  the 
many  flowers  Goethe  picked  for  her  in  Marienbad  in  1822  and 
1823.  Hard  as  it  may  seem  to  realize  it,  the  heroine  of  the 
Marienbad  elegy  is  still  among  the  living.  What  a  space  of 
time  between  the  buoyant  childhood  of  the  one  of  the  lovers 
when  Frederick  the  Great  was  in  his  prime  and  no  one  in  the 
American  colonies  was  yet  anticipating  an  independent  America, 
and  the  quiet  self-forgetful  old  age  of  the  other,  who  has  seen 
Bismarck  die  and  the  word  "  expansion  "  written  on  the  flag 
of  a  nation  that  has  been  stretching  from  ocean  to  ocean  for 
half  a  century!    And  yet  the  elegy  will  unite  them  for  all  time.

and  perhaps  never  more  beautiful  lines  will  be  written  on  the 
divineness  of  earthly  love  than  :

In  the  third  story,  which  was  inhabited  by  Goethe's  grand- 
sons, we  find  more  of  Goethe's  collections,  and  on  our  return  to 
the  ground  floor  we  may  see  a  new  work  of  sculpture,  by  Eber- 
lein,  representing  Goethe  with  the  skull  of  Schiller,  and  cast  a 
glance  at  the  old  trees  and  roses  Goethe  loved  so  dearly.  If  we 
imagine,  however,  now  to  have  made  a  hasty  examination  of  all 
the  treasures  of  the  museum  we  are  mistaken.  For  while  we 
may  have  caught  a  glimpse  of  most  of  the  casts  of  works  of 
sculpture,  the  paintings,  the  ancient  bronzes  and  gems,  the 
majolicas  and  the  physical  apparatus,  there  are  other  collections 
of  which  we  have  seen  little  or  nothing  because  their  size  or 
their  character  makes  it  necessary  to  reserve  them  for  the  inspec- 
tion of  the  special  student.  We  may  be  surprised  to  learn  that 
Goethe  possessed  150  silhouettes,  as  many  crayon  portraits,  and 
no  less  than  1600  historical  portrait  medallions,  among  them 
some  of  the  rarest  specimens  of  the  fifteenth  and  sixteenth  cen- 
turies ;  that  he  owned  2400  engravings,  woodcuts  and  litho- 
graphs, including  many  after  Mantegna,  Carracci,  Titian,  Diirer, 
Schongauer,  Rembrandt  and  Rubens  ;  1600  original  drawings, 
exclusive  of  some  hundreds  of  his  own,  among  which  such  by 
Guercino,  Guido  Reni,  Watteau,  Claude  Lorraine,  the  Poussins, 
Peter  Vischer  the  younger,  Rembrandt,  Van  Dyck  and  Carstens  ; 
and  1600  sulphur  and  plaster  casts  of  ancient  gems  ;  that  he  had 
collected  700  polished  samples  of  Roman  building  materials  and 
nearly  18,000  mineralogical  specimens,  and  in  addition  to  all 
these  things  built  up  a  private  library  of  some  5000  volumes. 
But  after  all  neither  the  quantity,  nor  indeed  the  intrinsic  quality

of  these  collections  would  lend  to  them  their  extraordinary

remain  unedited.

extant  autograph  of  hers  written  during  those  days  of  autumn 
1770.  The  other  twelve  drawings  belong  to  the  trip  to  Italy, 
and  show  the  rapid  progress  which  Goethe  made  in  this  art 
during  that  time.  The  character  and  "Stimmung"  of  Italian 
landscape  is  well  reproduced,  and  a  water-color  representing  a 
bay  in  Sicily  is  truly  exquisite.  Among  the  portraits  the  draw- 
ing of  Schwerdgeburth  for  his  well-known  engraving  of  Goethe 
stands  first.  Ii  was  done  in  1832,  and  is  not  only  the  last  but 
at  the  same  time  the  most  lofty  and  venerable  picture  of  the  old 
Goethe  we  possess.  Another  interesting  number  is  that  of  the 
young  Jerusalem.  The  second  publication  of  Ruland  is  entitled: 
Medallions  tmd  Bildnisse  des  Weimarischen  Kreises  i8oo-i8jo^

and  forms,  together  with  P.  v.  Bojanowski's  :  140  Jahre  Wei-

collections. 

However  interesting  the  Goethe-National-Museum  may  be, 
the  Goethe-  und  Schiller-Archiv  is  at  present  and  probably  will 
be  in  the  future  of  still  greater  importance  to  the  student  of  lit- 
erature. We  shall,  therefore,  give  a  fuller  description  of  it,  and 
dwell  at  greater  length  on  its  work.  The  building  itself,  with 
its  solid  material  and  its  four  columns  in  front,  presents  a  plain 
but  dignified  appearance.  Ascending  the  stone  staircase  from 
the  hall  on  the  ground  floor  we  reach  the  lofty  exhibition,  library, 
study  and  reception  rooms  in  the  second  story.  The  entrance 
to  the  central  exhibition  room  is  flanked  by  busts  of  Goethe  and 
Schiller,  works  of  the  Frankfurt  sculptor  Rumpf,  and  presented 
by  the  Goethe  Society.  This  room  and  the  adjoining  northwest 
room  contain  the  glass  cases  and  bookcases  with  the  treasures 
of  the  archives.  Of  the  glass  cases  of  the  first  room  two  exhibit 
documents  and  letters  referring  to  the  archives  and  its  protectress 
and  other  members  of  the  grand  ducal  house,  among  them  the 
concept  of  Goethe's  letter  of  congratulation  upon  the  birth  of 
Emperor  Frederick  ;  a  third  comprises  the  most  valuable  manu- 
scripts of  Goethe,  and  a  fourth  is  devoted  to  Schiller  and  Goethe 
together.  In  the  Goethe  case  there  is  Goethe's  prophetic  con- 
gratulation to  his  grandmother  on  the  New  Year  of  1757,  clos-

The  southwest  room  which  we  now  enter  is  occupied  by  the 
library  of  the  Goethe  Society.  It  numbers  over  4000  volumes, 
mainly  relating  to  Goethe,  though  lately  Schiller  and  several  of 
the  romantic  poets  have  received  some  attention.  It  is  destined 
for  the  labors  of  the  archives  and  for  the  members  of  the  Society. 
We  see  the  nineteen  volumes  of  the  Goethe-Jahrbuch^  the  twelve

and  the  letters  down  to  181 1.

Otto  Harnack's  volume  is  the  second  of  a  series  of  three  in 
which  all  of  Goethe's  writings  on  art  since  his  return  from 
Italy,  including  a  large  amount  of  unpublished  material,  are  so 
arranged  as  to  give  a  clearer  insight  into  the  growth  and  nature 
of  this  side  of  Goethe's  activity.  The  author  has  amply  quali- 
fied himself  for  the  task  by  extensive  study  of  the  subject 
and  a  long  stay  at  Rome.  While  he  merely  touched  upon 
it  in  his  inspiring  book  on  Goethe  in  der  Epoche  seiner  Vollen- 
dung^  he  dealt  with  it  more  or  less  closely  in  his  edition  of 
Goethes  Briefwechsel  mit  hreimden  und  Kiuisigenossen  in 
Italien^  lySS-iygo^  his  Klassische  Aesthetik  der  Deutschen  and 
his  Kitnstleben  in  Rom  im  Zeitalter  der  Klassik.  Only  his  latest 
work.  Life  of  Schiller^  which  reviewers  have  pronounced  the 
best  book  on  the  subject,  lies  almost  entirely  in  another  field. 
The  volume  in  question  brings  Goethes  Schriften  zur  Kunst^ 
1800-1816^  including  the  Maximen  nnd  Reflexionen  iiber  Knnst^ 
which,  though  not  all  written  during  that  period,  still  are 
inserted  here  becaiise  Goethe  had  desired  to  publish  them  col- 
lectively. The  order  of  the  volume  is  in  the  main  chronologi- 
cal. The  first  part  gives  all  the  Weimarische  Kiinstaiisstellun- 
gen  und  Preisaufgaben^  including  reproductions  of  the  works  of 
five  successful  competitors.  The  other  parts  contain  Goethe's 
studies  on  Polygnotus,  a  number  of  short  articles  and  Vorarbeiten 
und  Bruchstiicke.  As  for  the  treatise  on  Welcker's  Sapi)ho 
Kekule  has  not  inaptly  suggested  that  it  perhaps  remainea 
unpublished  in  deference  to  Wilhelm  von  Humboldt  and  his 
wife.  The  principal  difficulty  in  editing  the  volume  lay  in 
determining  the  respective  authorships  of  Goethe  and  Meyer  in 
the  case  of  articles  which  had  appeared  either  unsigned  or  under 
the  common  chiffre  of  Goethe  and  Meyer.  For  this  purpose 
Harnack  has  consulted  their  literary  estates  and  reached  results 
beyond  those  obtained  by  Weizsacker,  Witkowski  and  his  own 
former  efforts.  The  sense  of  community  of  work  was  so  strong 
with  Goethe  and  Meyer  that  neither  hesitated  to  refer  to  the

labors  of  the  other  as  his  own,  and  that  among  the  letters  only 
those  exchanged  between  them  can  bear  reliable  testimony  as  to 
the  authorship  of  either. 

Steiner's  Goethe's  Welta7ischauiing  must  have  been  a  surprise 
to  any  one  who  expected  from  its  title  to  find  anything  resem- 
bling Harnack's  Goethe  in  der  Epoche  seiner  Vollendung.  It  is 
explained,  however,  by  the  personality  and  the  previous  work  of 
the  author.  Steiner  is  the  author  of  Erkeiintnisthcorie  der  Goethe- 
schen  IVeltatiscJiaiinng  mit  bcsonderer  Rilcksicht  auf  Schiller^  the 
Philosphie  der  Freiheit^  Friedrich  Nietzsche^  ein  Kdmpfer  gegen 
seine  Zeit  and  other  philosophical  writings  and  the  editor  of  all, 
or  the  greater  part,  of  Goethe's  writings  on  natural  science  in 
the  Kiirschner  and  Weimar  editions.  In  his  work  on  the  latter 
he  has  won  wide  recognition  for  his  new  arrangement  and  pre- 
sentation of  the  material  so  vastly  increased  by  the  treasures  of 
the  archives.  He  is  a  clear  and  logical  and  at  the  same  time 
most  subjective  thinker,  for  he  wants  to  look  upon  another  per- 
sonality only  as  it  must  appear  to  him  according  to  his  own 
"  Wesenheit."  He  is  a  most  enthusiastic  admirer  of  Goethe  the 
naturalist,  and  yet  there  are  some  provinces  of  Goethe's  mind 
that  seemingly  fail  to  appeal  to  him.  Thus  he  tries  to  show  in 
the  book  under  consideration,  in  which  he  draws  the  sum  total 
of  all  his  previous  studies  of  Goethe's  views  on  nature  and  nat- 
ural science,  that  Goethe's  Weltanschaimng  appears  most  unim- 
paired where  it  is  freest  from  platonic  and  Christian  influences. 
However  little  many  of  his  readers  may  feel  inclined  to  agree  to 
this,  they  will  find  much  that  is  suggestive  and  well  worthy  of 
consideration,  and  will  respect  his  intrepid  love  of  truth  as  he 
sees  it.

Adolf  Gerber.  83 

often  the  place  of  argument.  In  his  earlier  treatise  Scheidemantel 
first  established  the  true  relation  of  the  two  Tasso  manuscripts 
of  the  archives,  and  then  showed  by  an  ingenious  combination  of 
the  fact  that  the  original  manuscript  has  Battista  in  the  place  of 
Antonio  in  Acts  IV,  1-5,  and  V,  with  arguments  resulting  from 
metrical  considerations  and  from  a  careful  examination  of 
Goethe's,[Herder's  and  Knebel's  correspondence  and  diaries,  that 
Goethe  began  the  work  on  the  final  v^ersion  with  some  scenes 
of  the  last  act  and  probably  ended  with  I,  4,  III,  2,  and  IV,  4 
and  5.  All  but  one  of  these  results  found  documentary  evidence 
by  the  copyist's  receipts,  which  had  been  preserved  in  the 
archives,  and  by  a  close  examination  of  the  various  kinds  of 
paper  and  wTiting  of  the  manuscript.  Thus  he  could  show  in 
the  Goethe  Jahrbiich  that  Goethe  finished  Acts  V  and  IV,  1-3, 
by  November,  1788 ;  Act  II,  2-5,  and  most  of  Act  III  in 
December  and  January  ;  Act  I,  1-3,  in  February  and  March  ; 
the  rest  of  Act  III  and  I,  4,  probably  in  March  and  April,  and 
Act  IV,  4-5,  and  perhaps  II,  i,  by  the  close  of  June. 

Dr.  Alt's  Studien  ziir  EntstcJmngsgeschichte  von  Goethe's 
Dichtiing  unci  Wahrheit^  which  have  appeared  as  part  V  of 
Muncker's  Forschungen  zur  neueren  Litteratui^geschichte^  do  not 
contain  such  startling  discoveries  as  Scheidemantel's  articles  on 
Tasso,  but  they  form  an  indispensable  supplement  to  the  studies 
of  Loeper  and  Diintzer,  and  to  the  critical  apparatus  by  Baech- 
told  jin  ^the^Weimar  edition,  which  is  corrected  by  it  in  some 
places.  The  young  author  has  separated  the  examination  of  the 
sources  from  the  discussion  of  the  composition  proper.  He  has 
found  that  Goethe  not  only  made  use  of  Bettina's  original  letters 
in  composing  Dichtiing  nnd  Wahrheit^  but  that  also  Bettina  in 
her  turn  drew  on  Dichtiing  und  Wahrheit  for  her  Briefwechsel 
Goethes  viit  einem  Kinde.  Not  the  Ehrengeddcht7iis^  as  Loeper 
and  Diintzer  had  supposed,  but  the  Diariiim  is  the  source  of  the 
account  of  the  coronation.  The  Frankfurter gelehrte  Anzeigen 
were  placed  more  under  contribution  than  the  Teutscher  Merkur 
or  even  the  Allge7neine  deutsche  Bibliothek.  No  less  than  thir- 
teen books  were  consulted  for  the  paragraph  on  the  Reichskam- 


84  The  Goethe  histitutions  at    Weimar. 

mcrgericht,  which  is  only  one  evidence  among  several  of  the 
very  great  care  which  Goethe  bestowed  on  important  facts  in  his 
autobiography.  In  tracing  the  composition  Dr.  Alt  avails  him- 
self of  all  the  material,  including  the  Biographische  Einzel- 
heitcn  and  the  tmpublished  diaries,  and  he  does  this  in  a  manner 
which  does  equal  credit  to  his  training  and  to  his  critical  facul- 
ties. He  shows  how,  as  a  rule,  Goethe  would  first  write  down 
something  in  which  he  was  specially  interested,  then  make  a 
synopsis  of  what  was  done  and  what  remained  to  be  done, 
thereupon  elaborate  the  material  and  finally  make  additions, 
transpositions  or  omissions,  as  the  case  might  be.  To  judge 
from  the  "  Schemata,"  the  fourth  volume  does  not  seem  ever  to 
have  been  entirely  completed,  and  this  appears  also  from  a  com- 
parison of  its  artistic  composition  with  that  of  the  preceding 
volumes.  The  author  closes  with  some  general  observations. 
Statements  in  the  autobiography  which  deviate  from  the  real 
facts  may  be  ascribed  to  three  main  reasons:  to  errors  of  memory 
with  regard  to  those  facts,  to  an  occasional  failure  to  revive  the 
original  impressions  of  facts  remembered,  and  to  the  poet's  not 
laying  stress  on  actual  happenings  if  they  were  unimportant, 
but  rather  on  the  influences  which  shaped  his  own  life  and  those 
of  his  contemporaries.  In  this  effort.  Dr.  Alt  thinks,  Goethe 
perhaps  went  so  far  as  consciously  to  invent  personages,  e.  g.^ 
the  officer  at  the  close  of  the  seventh  book,  the  Socratic  shoe- 
maker of  Dresden  and  the  daughters  of  the  Strassburg  dancing- 
master,  though,  as  the  beginning  of  Werther  proves,  a  similar 
love  affair  must  have  actually  occurred.  Some  slight  incongru- 
ities of  detail  are  easily  accounted  for  by  the  manner  in  which 
•the  work  was  elaborated.  The  task  of  artistic  composition  was 
an  exceedingly  difficult  one  owing  to  the  vast  designs  of  the 
poet.  If  in  some  places,  and  we  think  in  the  first  three  volumes 
they  are  comparatively  few,  the  artistic  form  seems  somewhat 
neglected  and  reflections  occupy  too  broad  a  space,  Dichtung 
imd  Wahrheit  shares  such  shortcomings  with  other  works  of 
Goethe's  old  age.

Kanzler  Miiller  is  still  considerably  more  valuable  than  the  first. 
Burkhardt,  the  director  of  the  State  archives — learned  people  in 
Weimar  sometimes  speak  of  a  Bnrkhardeum,  a  Suphaneum  and 
a  Rnlandeum — who  is  so  favorably  known  to  all  students  of 
Goethe  by  his  careful  critical  work,  offers  here  a  hundred  addi- 
tional conversations.  He  has  not  only  made  full  use  of  all  the 
information  in  the  Kanzler-Miiller-Archiv^  the  preservation  of 
which  is  largely  due  to  his  care,  but  has  compared  Goethe's 
diaries  as  well.  Comments  are  given  judiciously  but  sparingly 
in  order  to  keep  the  volume  within  reasonable  bounds.  A  full 
index  increases  its  usefulness  as  a  most  important  supplement  to 
Goethe's  diaries.  We  quote  an  utterance  of  Goethe's  with  regard 
to  his  relation  to  Christianity  from  April  9,  1830:  "Sie  wissen, 
wie  ich  das  Christentum  achte,  oder  Sie  wissen  es  vielleicht 
audi  nicht ;  wer  ist  denn  noch  heutzutage  ein  Christ,  wie 
Christus  ihn  haben  wollte  ?  Ich  allein  vielleicht,  ob  ihr  mich 
gleich  fiir  einen  Heiden  haltet." 

The  last  work  which  we  examine  is  :  Zum  24  Juiii  i8p8. 
Goethe  und  Maria  Paiilowna.  Festschrift  des  Goethe-  tind  Schil- 
ler-Archivs  heraiisgcgeben  im  Aiiftrage  des  Erbgrosshersogs 
Wilhehn  Ernst  von  Sachsen^  a  most  fitting  and  delicate  gift  to 
the  son  of  Maria  Paulowna  on  his  eightieth  birthday.  As  the 
book  is  not  in  the  trade,  some  notes  on  it  may  not  be  unwelcome. 
The  contents  are  as  follows  :  (I)  Poems;  (II)  Letters  of  Goethe 
to  Maria  Paulowna  and  of  Maria  Paulowna  to  Goethe  and  Schil- 
ler's wife ;  (III)  Evidences  of  common  activity ;  (IV)  The 
Weimar  art  friends;  (V)  Maria  Paulowna  in  utterances  of  Goethe, 
Schiller  and  other  contemporaries  ;  (VI)  "  Nachwirken  ;  "  (VII) 
Explanations  and  conclusion  by  Bernhard  Suphan.  The  first 
and  fifth  divisions  extend  to  the  birth  of  the  Grand  Duke  ;  the 
second,  third,  fourth  and  sixth  are  brought  further  down.  The 
poems  begin  with  the  verse  from  the  Epilogue  to  Schiller's 
Bell,  which  refers  to  the  Huldigiing  der  Kihiste^  by  which 
Schiller  at  once  won  the  heart  of  the  princess.  Among  the 
others  we  mention  the  beautiful  stanza  for  the  allium  of  Maria 
Paulowna,  which  ends  with  the  line  :  "  Die  schonsten  Krauze 


86  The  Goethe  Institictions  at   Wci7nar. 

winden  Lieb'  unci  Treue,"  and  the  stanzas  from  tlie  Maskenzug 
i8i8y  which  contain  a  welcome  and  a  blessing  for  the  present 
Grand  Duke,  then  in  his  cradle.  Among  the  letters  of  Goethe 
the  one  accompanying  the  Kurze  Vorstellung  der  Kantischen 
Philosophie  von  D.  F.  V.  i?.,  is  the  most  notable.  It  proves  that 
the  princess  was  a  lady  not  shrinking  from  "the  most  abstract 
things  which  man's  mind  may  hear  about  itself."  We  quote 
the  following:  "  Im  gemeinen  Leben  treibt  uns  die  Erfahrung 
auf  gewisse  Regeln  hin,  dem  Verstand  gelingt  es  zu  sondern,  zu 
vertheilen  und  nothdiirftig  zusammen  zu  stellen  und  so  entsteht 
eine  Art  von  Methode.  Nun  tritt  die  Vernunft  ein,  die  alles 
zusammenfasst,  sich  iiber  alles  erhebt,  nichts  vernachlassigt. 
Dazwischen  aberwird  unablassig  die  alles  durchdringende,  alles 
ausschmiickende  Phantasie  immer  reizender,  jemehr  sie  sich  der 
Sinnlichkeit  nahert,  immer  wiirdiger,  je  mehr  sie  sich  mit  der 
Vernunft  vereint.  hw  jener  Granze  ist  die  wahre  Poesie  zu 
finden,  hier  die  achte  Philosophie." 

The  other  letters  treat  of  the  construction  of  the  Weimar 
castle  and  Goethe's  superintendence  of  the  institutions  of  art 
and  science  in  Weimar  and  Jena,  to  which  Maria  Paulowna 
gave  not  only  personal  interest,  but  considerable  material  assist- 
ance. The  letters  of  Maria  Paulowna  show  her  regard  for 
Goethe  and  her  confidence  in  him.  The  evidences  of  common 
activity  are  articles,  letters  and  documents  referring  to  the  estab- 
lishment of  the  "  Freie  Gewerbschule  "  and  the  "  Lesemuseum," 
which  still  exist  to-day.  Suphan's  remarks  are  a  glowing 
tribute  to  Maria  Paulowna  and  furnish  an  explanation  as  to  why 
she,  though  rather  a  "  Schillersche  Natur,"  worked  in  such  har- 
mony with  Goethe  and  was  so  reverenced  by  him.  In  conclusion, 
Suphan  first  states  that  he  himself  had  formulated  the  plan  of 
the  book  and  had  superintended  the  composition  of  every  part 
of  it,  and  mentions  what  he  had  intended  to  do  and  what  Wahle, 
Fresenius  and  Schiiddekopf  had  contributed,  but  afterward  he 
endeavors  to  discard  all  "Selbstsinn"  and  the  small  "Ipse  feci," 
and  draws  a  picture  of  the  ideal  community  of  labor  in  the 
"Archive  "  where  everybody  is  glad  to  give  and  everybody  is  glad 
to  receive,  and  where  the  "  Mit  und  Ineinander  "  of  the  work  can 


Adolf  Gcrber.  87 

and  should  not  be  separated.  The  closing  line  is  :  "  Die  schon- 
sten  Kranze  winden  Lieb'  and  Treue. " 

There  still  remain  a  few  words  to  be  said  about  the  Goethe- 
Gesellschaft.  Though  its  membership  is  no  longer  so  large  as  it 
was  during  the  time  of  the  first  enthusiasm  over  the  new  avenues 
that  had  opened  for  the  study  of  Goethe,  it  still  has  over  twenty- 
six  hundred  members,  and  among  them  about  two  hundred  from 
England  and  America.  The  annual  fee  is  ten  marks,  for  which 
the  Goethe  Jahrbuch  and  the  current  xm.m}o^r  oi  Xh^  Schriften  der 
Goethe-Gesellschaft  are  furnished.  In  addition  to  this  a  special 
discount  is  allowed  on  the  Weimar  Goethe  edition. 

The  object  of  the  society  is  the  promotion  of  the  study  and 
the  appreciation  of  Goethe  by  means  of  the  creation  of  a  Goethe 
library,  the  purchase  of  manuscripts  or  works  of  art  for  the 
archives  and  the  museum,  the  publication  of  unprinted  material 
and  treatises,  and  annual  conventions  at  Weimar.  The  library 
and  the  last  number  of  the  ScJiriften  have  been  touched  upon. 
The  volumes  of  t\\Q.  Jahrbuch  for  1897  and  1898  contain,  besides 
the  article  of  Scheidemantel  and  a  reprint  of  the  letter  of  Goethe 
to  Maria  Paulowna,  with  the  abstract  of  Kant's  philosophy 
which  we  have  mentioned  above,  some  sixty  letters  from  the 
archives  edited  by  Fresenius,  Schiiddekopf,  Wahle  and  others, 
some  seventy  letters  or  parts  of  letters  from  other  sources,  a 
number  of  treatises,  a  great  deal  of  miscellaneous  matter,  obitu- 
aries of  the  Grand  Duchess  and  several  scholars,  reports  on  the 
Weimar  edition  and  the  Goethe  institutions,  and  the  address  of 
the  last  convention. 

This  convention  met  in  the  hall  of  the  "  Erholung,"  June  4. 
Both  on  the  evening  of  the  third  and  on  the  evening  of  the 
fourth  the  Court  Theatre  offered  a  special  treat:  Schiller's  Rduber^ 
with  the  famous  Lewinsky,  of  Vienna,  in  the  part  of  Franz 
Moor,  and  ballads  of  Schiller  and  Goethe  recited  by  the  same 
artist.  All  memorable  places  and  institutions  in  charge  of  the 
State  or  the  grand  ducal  house  were  freely  thrown  open.  The 
Grand  Duke  himself  and  members  of  his  family  attended  the 
entertainments  at  the  theatre  and  the  convention  proper,  and 
tendered  a  reception  to  some  of  those  present. 


88  The  Goethe  Institutioyis  at    Weimar. 

The  convention  was  called  to  order  by  Dr.  Ruland,  who  at 
the  end  of  his  opening  remarks  offered  to  those  interested  a  few 
copies  of  a  new  edition  of  Thomas  E.  Webb,  The  Tragedy  of 
Faust  in  Efiglisk^  inscribed  to  the  English  Goethe  Society 
and  to  the  Goethe-Gesellschaft  in  Weimar.  Whatever  the  merits 
of  this  translation,  which  omits  the  prelude  at  the  theatre  and 
adds  the  death  of  Faust  from  the  second  part,  may  be,  some  of 
the  comments  are  not  apt  to  meet  with  much  favor.  Working 
on  the  principle  that  "  when  a  great  writer  appears  to  be  absurd 
we  may  be  sure  he  is  not  understood,"  Webb  tries  to  overcome 
some  of  the  chronological  and  other  difficulties  by  maintaining 
that  Margaret  falls  in  the  "  Gartenhaus  "  of  Martha  ;  "  that  the 
night  of  the  assignation  in  the  Garden  is  the  night  of  the  duel 
on  the  Street,"  and  "  that  the  mother  and  the  son  expire  on  the 
same  night."  Also  the  common  error  that  Margaret  dies  in  the 
prison  is  repeated.  The  address  of  the  occasion  was  delivered 
by  Ulrich  von  Willamowitz-Mollendorf,  of  the  University  of 
Berlin.  His  subject  was  Goethe's  Pandora^  the  beautiful 
fragment  of  a  "  Vorspiel :  Pandoras  Wiederkunft,"  the  composi- 
tion of  which  the  poet  began  in  the  year  after  the  disaster  of 
Jena,  and  which  he  significantly  placed  at  the  close  of  the  last 
edition  of  his  works.  The  clue  of  the  play  was  sought  and 
found  in  the  lines  : 

Gross  beginnet  ihr  Titanen ;  aber  leiten 

Zu  dem  ewig  Gut  en,  ewig  Schonen 

1st  der  Gotter  Werk.     Die  lasst  gewahren. 

Science  and  art  are  the  mediators  between  earth  and  heaven. 
They  are  not  of  this  world  and  no  Napoleon  can  interfere  with 
their  realm.  Hence  the  poet  counsels  his  countrymen  to  rise  by 
devotion  to  science  and  art  above  the  sad  present,  as  he  had 
advised  them  to  restore  their  material  fortune  by  laboring  each 
in  his  own  sphere  in  the  Vorspiel  ziir  Erbffmiug  des  Weimari- 
schefi  Theaters  avi  ig  September  iSoy.  Particularly  new  and 
striking  was  the  discussion  of  Goethe's  sources.  They  are 
Hesiod,  Plato's  Protagoras,  the  services  of  Prometheus,  with 
the  olive  wreath,  and  of  Eros  in  the  bounds  of  the  Athenian 


Adolf  Gerber.  89 

Academy,  and  above  all  the  foundation  of  Plato's  academy,  the 
mother  of  all  following  institutions  of  pure  learning  in  that 
locality.  As  Plato  founded  his  school  in  a  State  shattered  by 
the  enemy,  Goethe  wrote  his  Pa?idora  amidst  the  ruins  of  the 
old  empire.  Pandora  herself  is  not  beauty,  but  corresponds 
to  loea^  forma,  and  may  most  easily  be  understood  by  Schiller's 
conception  of  the  "  Ideal." 

Among  the  rest  of  the  proceedings  the  announcement  of  the 
intention  to  compose  a  Goethe  dictionary,  with  the  co-operation 
of  members  of  the  society,  was  the  most  important.  Hermann 
Grimm  had  pronounced  in  favor  of  a  dictionary  including 
Goethe,  Schiller  and  Herder.  Erich  Schmidt  denounces  both 
the  extension  or  limitation  of  the  work  to  those  three  writers  and 
the  co-operation  of  untrained  contributors.  Conferences  con- 
cerning the  proposed  work,  in  which  Professor  Kluge  also  has 
taken  part,  have  not  yet  led  to  a  definite  result. 

The  few  things  which  it  has  been  possible  to  mention  within 
the  limits  of  this  article  are  intended  as  a  testimony  to  the  won- 
derful activity  of  the  Goethe  institutions,  and  to  the  importance 
which  Weimar  occupies  once  more  in  the  realm  of  letters,  even 
without  considering  the  ever  growing  fame  of  Friedrich 
Nietzsche.  In  closing  it  may  be  permitted  to  remark  that 
during  last  month  the  Weimar  Theatre,  with  the  aid  of  Son- 
nenthal  and  Wiecke,  gave  a  performance  of  the  entire  Wallen- 
stein  trilogy  in  commemoration  of  the  hundredth  anniversary  of 
the  first  representation  of  Wallensteins  Lager^  which  had  taken 
place  on  October  12,  1798,  in  honor  of  the  opening  of  the  new 
theatre.  That  "  new  theatre  "  was  burnt  and  the  present  house, 
which  has  replaced  it,  may  ere  long  give  way  to  a  more  stately 
one,  but  the  institution  is  faithful  to  the  great  traditions  of 
Goethe's  time  and  a  worthy  supplement  to  the  Goethe-National- 
Museum,  the  Goethe-  und  Schiller-Archiv  and  the  Goethe- 
Gesellschaft  of  Weimar. 

Adolf  Gerber.

BEITRAGE  ZUR  ERKI.XRUNG  VON   GOETHES  FAUST  II. 

IM  ANSCHLUSS  AN  DIE  AUSGABE  VON 

CAEVIN   THOMAS.* 


Seiner  Ausgabe  des  ersten  Teils  von  Goethes  Faust,  die  im  Jahre 
1892  erschien,  hat  Calvin  Thomas  kiirzlich  eine  Ausgabe  des  zweiten 
Teiles  folgen  lassen.  Ich  will  hier  nicht  untersuchen,  ob  die  letztere 
im  Hinblick  auf  die  vorziiglichen  Commentare  von  Eoeper  und 
Schroer  iiberhaupt  notig  war,  zuraal  der  englische  Leser,  der  den 
zweiten  Teil  von  Goethes  Faust  geniessen  will,  die  deutsche  Sprache 
gewiss  so  beherrschen  muss,  dass  ihm  das  Studium  jener  Commen- 
tare keine  Schwierigkeiten  mehr  bereiten  kann.  Professor  Thomas 
sagt  uns  jedoch  in  der  Vorrede,  dass  seine  Arbeit  von  Anfang  bis  zu 
Ende  aus  reiner  Liebe  zur  Sache  unternommen  worden  sei  und  als 
solche  verdient  sie  das  entschiedenste  Eob.  Seit  Bayard  Taylors 
glanzender  tjbersetzung  des  Faust,  die  mit  wertvollen,  nun  freilich 
vielfach  veralteten  Anmerkungen  begleitet  war,  ist  Thomas'  Ausgabe 
der  beste  Versuch,  englischen  Lesern  das  Gedicht  nahe  zu  bringen 
und  sie  vom  Standpunct  der  heutigen  Faustforscliung  aus  mit  den 
Problemen  bekannt  zu  machen,  die  dieser  vielgedeutete  Teil  der  Inter- 
pretation stellt. 

Wie  weit  Thomas  selbst  diese  Probleme  ihrer  Eosung  entgegenge- 
fiihrt  hat,  soil  die  nachstehende  Besprechung  zeigen.  Denn  ein 
Werk,  das  aus  Liebe  zur  Sache  unternommen  wurde,  wird  ja  die 
Kritik  wohl  nicht  ausschliessen ,  und  dem  Verfasser  selbst  kann  sicher 
nichts  unwillkommener  sein,  als  jene  wohlwollende,  gnadig  ein  Auge 
zudriickende  Nachsicht,  mit  der  in  Deutschland  oft  amerikanische 
Produkte  dieser  Art  besprochen  werden. 

Nach  der  Art,  in  der  die  Ausgaben  classischer  und  anderer  Dicht- 
werke  jetzt  gemacht  werden,  hat  Thomas  seine  Arbeit  in  zwei  Teile 
geteilt:  in  die  Einleitung,  die  dem  ganzen  Werke  vorausgeschickt 
ist,  und  in  die  Anmerkungen,  die  am  Ende  des  Buches  dem  Texte

and  Literatures,  Columbia  University,  Vol.  II:  The  Second  Part.  Boston:  D.  C. 
Heath  &  Co.,  1897. 

(90) 


Julius  Gocbcl.  91 

folgeii.  Die  Einleitimg  zerfallt  in  drei  Capital,  von  denen  das  erste 
liber  die  Entstehung  des  zweiten  Teiles  der  Faustdichtung  handelt. 
In  klarer  und  bestimmter  Weise  berichtet  Thomas  hier  die  bekannten 
Tliatsachen  tiber  die  allmalilige  Entstehung  unseres  Gedichtes  und 
gelegentlich  fiigt  er  hier  schon  seine  Auffasung  und  Auslegung 
gewisser  Teile  der  Dichtung  ein.  Doch  von  diesen  Interpretations- 
versuchen  wird  spater  die  Rede  sein. 

Im  zweiten  Capitel  der  Einleitung  bringt  der  Herausgeber  in 
schHchter  und  Hchtvoller  Erzahlung  eine  Wiedergabe  des  Inhalts  vom 
zweiten  Teil.  Hier  wie  im  ersten  Capitel  brauchte  Thomas  nur 
seinen  deutschen  Vorgangern  zu  folgen  um  sich  seiner  Aufgabe 
ehrenvoll  zu  entledigen. 

Das  dritte  Capitel  der  Einleitung  zerfallt  in  zwei  Teile.  Im  ersten 
giebt  Thomas  eine  kurze  Geschichte  der  friiheren  Interpretations- 
versuche  und  im  zweiten  Abschnitt  bespricht  er  die  didaktischen 
Elemente  unseres  Gedichtes.  Einem  deutschen  Leser  bietet  dieser 
letzte  Abschnitt  wohl  kaum  neue  Gedanken,  aber  mit  Vergniigen 
wird  er  aus  dem  Munde  eines  Amerikaners  horen,  dass  es  nicht 
der  Zweck  der  Poesie  und  besonders  nicht  die  Absicht  des  Faust- 
Dramas  sei,  Moral  zu  lehren.  Mit  Recht  betont  Thomas,  dass 
Goethes  poetisches  Denken  sich  in  Bildern  und  nicht  in  schemen- 
haften  Abstraktionen  vollzieht,  fiir  die  es  die  Verkorperung  sucht. 
Allein  er  geht  auf  alle  Falle  zu  weit,  wenn  er  fiir  den  zweiten  Teil 
des  Faust  das  Vorkommen  von  Allegorien  leugnet.  Nach  seinen 
Bemerkungen  zu  schliessen,  scheint  Thomas  den  fundamentalen 
Unterschied  zwischen  Allegoric  und  S3^mbol  gar  nicht  gefasst  zu 
haben  und  ich  mochte  ihm  daher  fiir  eine  zukiinftige  Auflage  die 
classische,  in  vieler  Hinsicht  abschliessende  Abhandlung  "  Das 
Symbol  "  von  Vischer  empfehlen.  Denn  ich  halte  es  fiir  die  Inter- 
pretation des  zweiten  Teiles  von  allergrosster  Bedeutung,  dass  man 
sich  iiber  den  Begriff  des  Symbols  und  der  Allegoric,  der  fiir  die 
Wertbestimmung  des  dichterischen  Verfahrens  und  seiner  Produkte 
so  wichtig  ist,  vor  allem  klar  werde.  Zu  leugnen,  dass  Goethes 
Dichten  nicht  oft  auch  vom  Gedanken  ausging,  kann  nur  der 
Unkenntnis  einfallen.  So  schreibt  er  schon  im  Jahre  1771  an  Her- 
der, dem  er  seinen  Plan  zu  einer  Sokrates-Tragodie  entwickelt: 
"Ich  brauche  Zeit,  das  (d.  h.  die  blossen  Gedanken)  2zun  Gefuhl 
zu  entwickeln .' '  Und  mit  innigstem  Verstandnis  der  geheimen  Vor- 
gange  in  der  Dichterseele  schreibt  Schiller  in  dem  beriihmten  Briefe 


92  Beitriige  ziir  Erklamng  vo7i   Goethes  Faust  II. 

vom  23.  August,  1794,  indem  er  "  die  Summe  von  Goethes  Exist- 
enz  zieht,"  an  diesen:  "  Sie  haben  also  eine  Arbeit  mehr,  denn  so, 
wie  Sie  von  der  Anschauung  zur  Abstraktion  iibergehen,  so  mussten 
Sie  nun  riickwarts  Begriffe  wieder  in  Intuitionen  umsetzen  und 
Geda7iken  in  Gefiihle  verwa7ideln,  weil  nur  durch  diese  das  Genie 
hervorbringen  kann." 

Der  Umwandlungsprocess  von  Gedanke  in  Gefiihl,  der  somit  fiir 
Goethe  feststeht,  vnid  gerade  bei  der  Arbeit  am  zweiten  Teile  von 
Faust  durch  die  vielen  Entwiirfe  und  Schemata  bezeugt  ist,  wird  von 
Vischer  nach  Volkels  Vorgang  mit  "  Einfuhlung  "  bezeichnet.  Wer 
aber  mochte  behaupten,  dass  dem  alternden  Dichter  die  warme 
"  Einfiihlung  "  iiberall  gegliickt  sei,  und  dass  nicht  einzehie  Gestal- 
ten  des  zweiten  Teils  bei  der  frostigen  Allegoric  stehen  blieben  ? 
Gewiss  hat  Thomas  Recht,  wenn  er  sagt,  dass  die  Symbolik  eines 
Dichtwerks  nicht  auf  dem  Wege  der  logischen  Analyse  sondern 
durch  die  Phantasie  zu  verstehen  sei.  Aber  Phantasie  allein  thut's 
nicht,  sondern  das  Gefiihl  muss  hinzukommen.  Denn  die  Phantasie 
kann  audi,  wie  verschiedene  Romantiker  bezeugen,  recht  wohl  mit 
dem  diirrsten  Verstand  eine  Ehe  schliessen,  aber  erst  das  gefichlte 
Bild  ist  wahre  Poesie.  Und  wahre  Poesie  versteht  nicht,  wer  dieses 
Gefiihles  entbehrt. 

Ich  habe  die  verstandige  Klarheit  schon  hervorgehoben,  mit  der 
Thomas  seinen  Gegenstand  im  Ganzen  anfasst.  Es  wirkt  in  der 
That  erfrischend,  zu  sehen,  wie  vorteilhaft  hierin  die  Antange  ameri- 
kanischer  Faustforschung  von  den  Abstrusitaten  der  ersten  deutschen 
Versuche  sich  unterscheiden.  Sein  wohlgemeinter  Rationalismus 
lasst  Thomas  jedoch  im  Stich,  wenn  er  es  nicht  mit  den  Meinungen 
Anderer  zu  thun  hat,  ja  er  wird  sogar  zur  Oberflachlichkeit,  sobald 
er  uns  seine  eigenen  Interpretationen  giebt.  Denn  so  muss  ich  es 
nennen,  wenn  Thomas  in  dem  Streben,  anerkannte  Schwierigkeiten 
leicht  zu  behandeln,  wichtige  poetische  Motive  und  Erfindungen,  wie 
z.  B. ,  "  die  Miitter, "  als  "a  bit  of  solemn  fooling  ' '  erklart.  Er  liihlt 
sich  zu  dieser  Auffassung  der  Miitter  berechtigt,  weil  Goethe  auf 
Eckermanns  Bitte,  um  naheren  Aufschluss  iiber  die  Scene,  diesen 
mit  grossen  Augen  anblickte  und  die  Worte  wiederholte: 

"  Die  Miitter!  Miitter!  's  klingt  so  wunderlich!" 

Hatte  Thomas  bei  Eckermann  weiter  gelesen  und  hatte  er  vor 
allem  bedacht,  wie  Goethe  es  grundsatzlich  ablehnte,  Phantasiepro- 
dukte  auf  eine  verstandesmassige  Form  zu  bringen,  dann  ware  er 


Julius  Goebel.  93 

niclit  auf  eine  Auslegung  verfallen,  die  im  Gruiide  besagt,  dass  der 
Ausleger  mit  den  Miittern  einfach  nichts  anzufangen  wusste. 

Die  alteren  Deutungen  dieser  Scene,  selbst  die  am  wenigsten 
abstruse  Ch.  H.  Weisses  sind  natiirlich  ganz  aus  dem  Spiele  zu 
lassen.  Auch  Loeper  und  Schroer  bringen  im  Grunde  nur  wenig  zu 
ihrem  Verstandnisse  bei.  Nach  Eckermanns  und  Riemers  Zeugnis 
(Riemer,  Mitthl.  iiber  Goethe  i,  396)  ist  Goethe  die  Anregung  zu 
den  Miittern  aus  Plutarch  gekommen.  Es  muss  hier  jedoch  erwahnt 
werden,  dass  sich  die  Vorstellung  von  den  Miittern  auch  im  Deut- 
schen  findet.  Nicht  nur  ist  in  der  Bergmannssprache  von  den 
"  Erzmiittern  "  die  Rede  (D.  W.  B.  IV,  1229).  Bei  Brockes  (I, 
302)*  erscheinen  die  Miitter  in  der  Erde  Tiefen  und  der  zeugende, 
sie  schwangernde  Vater  ist  der  Weltgeist  (Brockes  I,  300). f  Viel- 
leicht,  dass  diese  Vorstellung  ihre  biblische  Ankniipfung  in  der  Stelle 
Psalm  139,  15  findet:  es  war  dir  mein  Gebein  nicht  verhohlen,  da 
ich  im  Verborgenen  gemacht  ward,  da  ich  gebildet  ward  unten  iii  der 
Erde. 

Ob  Goethe  nun  mit  der  deutschen  Vorstellung  der  Miitter  bekannt 
war  oder  nicht,  zweierlei  scheint  ihn  veranlasst  zu  haben,  von  dem 
Mythus  Gebrauch  zu  machen.  Zunachst  musste  er,  falls  er  die 
streng  durchgefuhrte  Charakteristik  des  Teufels  als  eines  christlich- 
mittelalterlichen  Wesens  nicht  zerstoren  wollte,  auf  ein  anderes  Mittel 
sinnen,  um  die  Beschworung  der  Helena  ins  Werk  zu  setzen.  Zu 
sagen,  dass  Mephisto,  ganz  wieim  Faustbuch,  das  Zauberkunststiick 
der  Beschworung  selbst  hatte  ausfiihren  konnen,  heisst  eben  Goethes 
Absichten  bei  der  Gegeniiberstellung  nordischer  und  classischer 
Geisterwelt,  die  ja  im  ganzen  zweiten  Teil  mit  tiefem  Humor  durch- 
getiihrt  ist,  ganzlich  misverstehen,  Der  Mythus  von  den  Miittern 
schlug  aber  darum  so  sehr  bei  Goethe  ein,  weil  sich  in  diesem  Bilde 
ein  Teil  seines  tiefsten  Denkens  zu  verdichten  und  symbolisch  darzu- 
stellen  schien.

Es  ist  lacherlich,  zu  vermuten,  dass  Faust  liier  erschrecke  und

ist  doch  klar  genug.  Er,  der  von  den  klassischen  Miittern  gewiss

seines  Kindes  nicht  einfiele.  Und  die  gewaltige  Erschiitterung

Doch  im  Erstarren  such  ich  nicht  mein  Heil.

Wie  auch  die  Welt  ihm  das  Gefuhl  vertheure, 
Ergriffen,  fiihlt  er  tief  das  Ungeheure. 

"  Menschheit  "  bedeutet  hier,  wie  in  der  Stelle: 

Der  Menschheit  ganzer  Jammer  fasst  mich  an

umgestaltenden  Naturschaffens  darstellen.  Und  wie  ein  Commentar

Wie  Thomas  dazu  kommen  konnte,  eine  Scene  als  "a  bit  of  solemn

Ich  wahle,  da  es  meine  Aufgabe  nicht  ist,  einen  vollig  neuen  Com-

Zunachst  der  Monolog  Fausts  am  Anfang  des  zweiten  Teils.  Das 
Ganze,  ausserlich  betrachtet,  die  Schilderung  eines  herrlichen  Son-

Weisen,

Auch  an  Schiller's  "  Verschleiertes  Bild  zu  Sais  "  ist  zu  erinnern  und

Der  Schleier  in  unserer  Stelle  ist  also  gleich  dem  Schein,  von  dem

die  Wahrheit  von  der  das  Licht  quillt.  Die  Nacht  ist  die  Unwahr-

Ddmmeriing :  eine  Geburt  von  Wahrheit  und  Un wahrheit. 

Doch  genug.  Urn  den  Sinn  des  Monologs  noch  einmal  zusammen-

steht  dieser  Monolog  am  Anfang  des  zweiten  Teils.  Noch  einmal,

Ahnen  der  Jugend.  Aber  es  ist  nicht  schwache  Resignation  allein, 
nicht  nur  matter  Cikadensprung.  In  ihm  wachst  zugleich  die  frohe 
Krkenntnis,  dass  er  das  ewige  Licht  doch  haben  kann  in  seinem 
herrlichen  Abglanz.  Wir  diirfen  bei  seiner  rastlos  strebenden 
Natur  hoffen,  dass  er  sich  dem  farbigen  Abglanz  nun  wirklich 
zuwenden  werde  um  das  wahre  Leboi  zu  finden.  Denn  das  Such  en 
und  endliche  Finden  des  wahren  Lebens  ist  ja  schliesslich  das  eigent-

miissen  zwischen  wahrem  und  falschern  Schein  zu  scheiden,  er 
wird  das  Zauberwesen  am  kaiserlichen  Hofe  wie  den  ganzen  klassi-

wahren  Abglanz  des  Kwigen  zu  finden: 

"So  im  Kleinen  wie  im  Gro.ssen

Sind  ein  Abglanz  jenes  Urlichts  d^vbeti,

Ich  setze  nun  hierher,  was  Thomas  zur  Erlauterung  unserer  Scene 
zu  sagen  hat:  Faust  sees  in  the  ever-changing  rainbow,  which  is  the 
"colored  reflection"  of  our  ever- varying  conditions,  a  symbol 
of  human  life.  The  essence  of  the  analogy  is  that  life  takes  its 
character  from  its  surroundings.  The  white  light  of  ideal  purpose  ( !) 
(Bestreben)  manifests  itself  only  as  it  breaks  upon  and  is  reflected 
back  in  visible  colors  by  the  facts  of  life,  etc.

Als  Goethe  das  Helena-Drama  vollendete,  hatte  er  die  antik-klas-

wichtige,  aber  immerhin  doch  niir  eine  Episode.  Der  Dichter  des 
zweiten  Teils  von  Faust  war  nicht  mehr  der  Dichter  der  Romischen 
Elegien  und  der  Achilleis.  Nur  der  gelehrte  Schulstandpunkt,  der 
in  der  griechisch-romischen  Bildung  das  Hochste  sieht  und  von 
Goethes  innerer  Abkehr  vom  rein  Klassischen  nichts  weiss,  wird 
darum  aus  der  Helenaepisode  fiir  die  Entwicklung  Fausts  soviel  zu 
machen  suchen  oder  sie,  weil  das  Helenadrama  zufallig  in  der  Mitte 
des  zweiten  Teils  steht,  in  den  Mittelpunkt  der  Erklarung  stellen.

Ungelostes  aufs  leichteste.  Vor  allem,  dass  Faust  der  Geliebten  und 
seinem  Kinde  audi  nicht  eine  Thrane  nachweint.  Ware  das  Erlebnis  . 
von  so  grosser  Bedeutung  fiir  sein  inneres  L,eben,  dann  ware  es

sprochene  Sinnlichkeit  hervorragender  Menschen  dadurch  zu 
erklaren,  dass  es  im  Wesen  starker  Geisteskraft  liege,  aus  der  Welt 
der  Materie  alles  Erreichbare  heranzuziehen.  Und  wie  der  Geist 
hier  als  ein  von  den  Elementen  unabhangiges  Wesen  erscheint, 
so  auch  in  dem  nachstehenden  Spruch  "Aus  Gott,  Gemiith  und 
Welt": 

Und  wer  durch  alle  die  Elemente, 

Feuer,  Euft,  Wasser  und  Erde  rennte, 

Der  wird  zuletzt  sich  iiberzeugen, 

Er  sei  kein  Wesen  Hires  Gleicheyi. 

Wie  es  aber  zu  denken  sei,  dass  die  ewigeLiebe  allein  es  vermoge,

Gedichtes  ' '  Hoheres  und  Hochstes ' '  im  West-ostlichen  Divan  klar 
das  auch  sonst,  weil  es  Goethe's  Denken  iiber  das  Eeben  nach  dem 
Tode  charakterisiert,  fiir  die  letzte  Scene  im  Faust  heranzuziehen  ist, 
Der  Dichter  denkt  sich  schon  im  Paradiese,  wo  er  sicher  ist,  dass  er

Auch  die  "  Flocken  "  (v.  1 1985)  sind  nicht  richtig  verstanden. 
Thomas' erklart:  "  Flocken,  used  loosely,  it  would  seem,  in  the

matter  is,  at  best,  somewhat  unimaginable,  but  Schroer  is  surely 
wrong  in  thinking  of  '  Flocken  '  as  a  garment  which  is  to  be  taken 
oflf." 

Das  Bild  von  der  Puppe  ist  Goethe  gelaufig,  so  z.  B.  im  Gedichte 
"  Ilmenau:" 

Wer  kann  der  Raupe,  die  am  Zweige  kriecht. 

Von  ihrem  kiinft,  gen  Futter  sprechen? 

Und  wer  der  Puppe,  die  am  Boden  liegt. 

Die  zarte  Schale  helfen  durchzubrechen  ? 

Vergleicht  er  sicli  nun  am  Knde  seines  Lebens  selbst  der  Raupe, 
die  sich  einzuspinnen  eilt  (Sprichwortlich): 

' '  Warum  magst  du  gewisse  Schriften  nicht  lesen  ?  ' ' 
Das  ist  auch  sonst  meine  Speise  gewesen ; 
Kilt  aber  die  Raupe  sich  eiriziispinnen, 
Nicht  mag  sie  mehr  Blattern  Geschmack  abgewinnen, 

dann  lasst  sich  verstehen,  wie  er  auch  Fausts  Zustand  in  diesem

cocon,  abzulosen,  damit  Faust  dem  Schmetterling  gleich  "  schon  und

sich  den  iiberirdischen  Vorgang  an  Faust  vorzustellen,  allein  wir

diirfen  nicht  vergessen,  dass  Goethe  doch  niir  in  irdischen  Bildern 
von  himmlischen  Geschehnissen  reden  oder  vielmehr  ' '  stammeln  ' ' 
konnte.  Und  diese  Bilder  ilim  nachzudenken,  ist  eben  die  Pflicht 
des  Interpreten, 

Zum  Schlusse  bedarf  der  Chorus  mysticus  nocli  besserer  Erklarung 
als  Thomas  ihn  wiirdig  halt,  indem  er  die  recht  hiibschen,  aber 
kleineswegs  ganz  zutreflfenden,  geschweige  denn  erschopfenden 
Worte  Bayard  Taylor' s  citiert :  ' '  lyOve  is  the  all-uplifting  and  all- 
redeeming  power  on  Earth  and  in  Heaven;  and  to  Man  it  is  revealed 
in  its  more  pure  and  perfect  form  through  Woman.  Thus,  in  the 
transitory  life  of  Earth,  it  is  only  a  symbol  of  its  diviner  being;  the 
possibilities  of  Love,  which  Earth  can  never  fulfill,  become  realities 
in  the  higher,  life  which  follows;  the  Spirit,  which  Woman  interprets 
to  us  here,  still  draws  us  upward  (as  Margaret  draws  the  soul  of 
Faust)  there."  Auch  hier  gilt  es,  den  Tiefsinn  der  Strophe  aus 
Goethes  Gedankenwelt  zu  erlautern  und  dem  Standpunkt  des  Dich- 
ters  ge recht  zu  werden,  der,  iiber  die  Welt  der  Erscheinungen 
erhoben  aus  himmlischen  Regionen  sein  letztes  und  hochstes  Schauen 
uns  gleichsam  zujauchzt. 

Wie  tief  aber  symbolisches  Denken,  das  hier  die  ganze  vergang- 
liche  Erscheinungswelt  als  Gleichnis  nimmt,  im  Geiste  Goethes 
wurzelt,  davon  zeugt  eine  Reihe  von  Ausspriichen.  So  fasst  er  friih 
schon  sein  Leben  symbolisch  auf,  indem  er  im  Jahre  1777  an  Frau 
von  Stein  schreibt:  "  Sie  wissen,  wie  symbohsch  mein  Dasein  ist," 
und  besonders  in  spateren  Jahren  kommt  er  ofter  auf  das  Wesen  und 
den  Wert  der  Symbolik  zu  sprechen.  In  denSpriichen  in  ProsaNo. 
743  lasst  er  sich  dariiber  also  aus:  "  Die  Symbolik  verwandelt  die 
Erscheinung  in  Idee,  die  Idee  in  ein  Bild,  und  so,  dass  die  Idee  im 
Bild  immer  unendlich  wirksam  und  unerreichbar  bleibt  und  selbst  in 
alien  Sprachen  ausgesprochen  doch  unaussprechlich  bliebe. ' ' 

Es  ist  begreiflich,  wie  ihm  bei  seinem  gegenstandlichen  Denken  die 
Manifestationen  der  ewig  einen  Idee  (Spr.  in  Pr.  334)  auch  ohne 
Einfluss  von  Kant,  den  L,oeper  vermutet,  zum  Gleichnis  des  Unend- 
lichen,  Unaussprechlichen  werden  mussten.  So  heisst  es  in  der 
Einleitung  zum  Versuch  einer  Witterungslehre  vom  Jahre  1825: 
Das  Wahre,  mit  dem  Gottlichen  identisch,  lasst  sich  niemals  von  uns 
direkt  erkennen:  wir  schauen  es  nurim  Abglanz,  im  Beispiel,  Symbol. 
Und  ganz  ahnlich  181 9:  Natura  infinitaest,  sed  qui  symbola  animad- 
verterit  omnia  intelliget  licet  non  omnino.

Und  nach  jenen  Gefilden,  wo  in  seliger  Vollendung  thront,  was  wir

des  Menschlichen  gedacht,  dessen   reinstes  Wesen   gewiss    in  der

Liebe  sich  ausdriickt.  Wie  aber  Goethe  das  "  Hinanziehen  " 
meinte,  das  wurde  sclion  am  Anfang  des  4.  Aktes  klar,  wo  Gretchens

Goethes,  dass  die  wahre  Liebe  im  Manne  das  Beste  und  Idealste 
seiner  Natur  aufruft  und  ihn  himmelwarts  zieht,  im  Eingang  der 
Elegie : 

Kein  Zweifel  mehr!     Sie  tritt  ans  Himmelsthor, 

In  ihren  Armen  hebt  sie  dich  empor. 

Meine  Besprechung  der  Thomaschen  Ausgabe  hat  sich  absichthch 
an  die  wichtigen  Fragen  der  Interpretation  gehalten  und  Nebensach- 
Hcheres,  wie  Textbehandlung,  tjbersetzungen  von  Einzelstellen, 
Metrisches,  etc.,  bei  Seite  gelassen,  obwohl  auch  hier  wie  in  Thomas' 
Ausgabe  von  Faust  I  viel  zu  berichtigen  ware.*  Wo  es,  wie  wir  an 
der  vorliegenden  Ausgabe  gesehen  haben,  an  tieferem  Verstandnis 
einer  freihch  schwierigen  Dichtung  so  sehr  hapert,  da  ist  es  eben 
zunachst  Pflicht  der  Kritik,  dem  Verstandnis  nachzuhelfen.  Und 
ist  es  schUesslich  nicht  aller  Interpretation  leztes  Ziel,  dem  Dichter 
das  Geschaifene  nachzuschafFen  ?  Freihch  geniigt  dazu  nicht, 
moghchst  viel  gelehrt  sein  sollendes  Material  von  aussen  her  auf 
schwierige  Stellen  zu  thiirraen,  vielmehr  gilt,  was  Goethe  zum 
Verstandnis  seiner  Helena  sagt  (Riemer  2,  581)  auch  von  der  Inter- 
pretation des  ganzen  zweiten  Teiles:  Von  einer  Seite  wird  dem 
Philologeyi  niclits  geheimes  bleiben,  er  wird  sich  vielmehr  an  dem 
wiederbelebten  Alterthum,  das  er  schon  kennt  ergotzen,  von  der 
andern  Seite  wird  ein  Fuhlender  dasjenige  durchdringen,  was  ge- 
miitlich  hie  und  da  verdeckt  liegt.  .  .  .  Auch  wegen  anderen 
dunkleren  Stellen  in  friihern  und  spatern  Gedichten  mochte  ich 
Folgendes  zu  bedenken  geben.  Da  sich  gar  manches  unserer  Erfah- 
rungen  nicht  rtind  aussprechen  und  direkt  mitteilen  lasst,  so  habe  ich 
seit  langem  das  Mittel  gewahlt,  durch  einander  gegeniibergestellte 
und  sich  gleichsam  in  einander  abspiegehide  Gebilde  den  geheimeren 
Sinn  dem  Aufmerkenden  zu  offenbaren. ' ' 

Es  ist  hohe  Zeit,  dass  die  Faust-  und  Goethe- Commentatoren  dies 
' '  Aufmerken  ' '  durch  Vertiefung  in  des  Dichters  Gedanken-  und 
Gefiihlswelt  lernen  und  vor  Allem  neben  Philologen  auch  Fiihlende 
werden.  Julius  Goebel,.
